903

904 <section id="_242">

905

906 <h2>

907General Information About Merkel Cell Carcinoma</h2>

908

909 <div id="_section_242" class="pdq-sections">

910<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

911<h6>

912In This Section</h6>

913<ul>

914<li>

915<a href="#_345_toc">

916Anatomy</a>

917</li>

918<li>

919<a href="#_246_toc">

920Epidemiology/Etiology</a>

921</li>

922<li>

923<a href="#_346_toc">

924Incidence and Mortality</a>

925</li>

926<li>

927<a href="#_347_toc">

928Pathogenesis</a>

929</li>

930<li>

931<a href="#_256_toc">

932Clinical Presentation</a>

933</li>

934<li>

935<a href="#_261_toc">

936Initial Clinical Evaluation</a>

937</li>

938<li>

939<a href="#_265_toc">

940Initial Staging Results</a>

941</li>

942<li>

943<a href="#_268_toc">

944Clinical Progression</a>

945</li>

946<li>

947<a href="#_272_toc">

948Potential Prognostic Factors</a>

949</li>

950<li>

951<a href="#_278_toc">

952Prognosis</a>

953</li>

954</ul>

955</nav>

956<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

957<a href="/types/skin/patient/merkel-cell-treatment-pdq">

958Go to Patient Version</a>

959</div>

960<p id="_244" tabindex="-1">

961Merkel cell carcinoma (MCC) was originally described by Toker in 1972 as trabecular carcinoma of the skin.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9621</a>

963] Other names include Toker tumor, primary small cell carcinoma of the skin, primary cutaneous neuroendocrine tumor, and malignant trichodiscoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9642</a>

965]</p>

966<p id="_245" tabindex="-1">

967MCC is an aggressive neuroendocrine carcinoma arising in the dermoepidermal junction (see <a href="/types/skin/hp/merkel-cell-treatment-pdq#_152">

968Figure 1</a>

969), and it is the second most common cause of skin cancer death after melanoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9703</a>

971,<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

9724</a>

973] Although the exact origin and function of the Merkel cell remains under investigation, it is thought to have features of both epithelial and neuroendocrine origin and arise in cells with touch-sensitivity function (mechanoreceptors).[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

9745</a>

975-<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

97611</a>

977]</p>

978<p id="_395" tabindex="-1">

979Therapeutic options have been historically limited for patients with advanced disease; however, new immunotherapeutic approaches are associated with durable responses.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

98012</a>

981]</p>

982<section id="_345">

983<h3 id="_345_toc">

984Anatomy</h3>

985<p id="_350" tabindex="-1">

986<figure id="figure_152" class="image-center-medium">

987<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/579043.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

988Enlarge</a>

989<img id="_152" alt="MerkelCell" title="MerkelCell" src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/579043-750.jpg">

990<figcaption class="caption-container">

991Figure 1. Merkel Cell Anatomy.</figcaption>

992</figure>

993</p>

994</section>

995<section id="_246">

996<h3 id="_246_toc">

997Epidemiology/Etiology</h3>

998<p id="_247" tabindex="-1">

999In Surveillance, Epidemiology, and End Results (SEER) Program data from 1986 to 2001, the age-adjusted U.S. annual incidence of MCC tripled from 0.15 to 0.44 per 100,000, an increase of 8.08% per year. Although this rate of increase is faster than any other skin cancer including melanoma, the absolute number of U.S. cases per year is small. About 1,500 new cases of MCC were expected in the United States in 2007.[<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

100013</a>

1001-<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

100218</a>

1003]</p>

1004</section>

1005<section id="_346">

1006<h3 id="_346_toc">

1007Incidence and Mortality</h3>

1008<p id="_248" tabindex="-1">

1009MCC incidence increases progressively with age. There are few cases in patients younger than 50 years, and the median age at diagnosis is about 65 years (see <a href="/types/skin/hp/merkel-cell-treatment-pdq#_240">

1010Figure 2</a>

1011).[<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

101214</a>

1013] Incidence is considerably greater in White individuals than in Black individuals and slightly greater in men than in women.[<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

101413</a>

1015-<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

101616</a>

1017,<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

101818</a>

1019] <figure id="figure_240" class="image-center">

1020<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/658575.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1021Enlarge</a>

1022<img id="_240" alt="MerkelCell" title="MerkelCell" src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/658575-750.jpg">

1023<figcaption class="caption-container">

1024Figure 2. Frequency of MCC by age and sex of men (square) and women (circle). Reprinted from Journal of the American Academy of Dermatology, 49 (5), Agelli M and Clegg L, Epidemiology of primary Merkel cell carcinoma in the United States, pp. 832&#8211;41, Copyright (2003), with permission from Elsevier. </figcaption>

1025</figure>

1026</p>

1027<p id="_250" tabindex="-1">

1028MCC occurs most frequently in sun-exposed areas of skin, particularly the head and neck, followed by the extremities, and then the trunk.[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

10295</a>

1030,<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

103116</a>

1032,<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

103319</a>

1034] Incidence has been reported to be greater in geographic regions with higher levels of ultraviolet B sunlight.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

103516</a>

1036] </p>

1037<p id="_396" tabindex="-1">

1038As of 2013, MCC had an annual incidence of 0.7 per 100,000 in the United States.[<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

103920</a>

1040] The incidence has been increasing over the past several decades, almost doubling in the United States between 2000 and 2013. This rise is potentially related to more accurate diagnostic pathology tools, improved clinical awareness of MCC, an aging population, increased sun exposure in susceptible populations, and improved registry tools. The incidence is also higher in immunosuppressed populations (HIV, hematologic malignancies, immunosuppressive medications, etc.).[<a href="#cit/section_1.21">

104121</a>

1042] Approximately 25,000 cases of MCC have been recorded in the United States since 2000, including more than 2,200 incident cases reported in 2014 to the National Program of Cancer Registries/SEER combined registries, which captures more than 98% of the U.S. population and the ten most common sites of MCC (see <a href="/types/skin/hp/merkel-cell-treatment-pdq#_215">

1043Table 1</a>

1044).[<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

104518</a>

1046]</p>

1047<table id="_215" class="table-default expandable-container">

1048<caption>

1049Table 1. The Ten Most Common Sites for Merkel Cell Carcinoma (SEER 1973&#8211;2006)<span class="sup">

1050a</span>

113731 (0.8)</td>

1138</tr>

1139<tr>

1140<td>

1141<strong>

1142Total</strong>

1143</td>

1144<td>

1145<strong>

11463,804 (98.3)</strong>

1147</td>

1148</tr>

1149</tbody>

1150</table>

1151<p id="_252" tabindex="-1">

1152In various cases series, up to 97% of MCCs arise in the skin. MCC primaries in other sites were very rare, as were MCCs from unknown primary sites.[<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

115318</a>

1154]</p>

1155<p id="_253" tabindex="-1">

1156SEER registry data have shown excess risk of MCC as a first or second cancer in patients with several primary cancers.[<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

115722</a>

1158] National cancer registries from three Scandinavian countries have identified a variety of second cancers diagnosed after MCC.[<a href="#cit/section_1.23">

115923</a>

1160] </p>

1161</section>

1162<section id="_347">

1163<h3 id="_347_toc">

1164Pathogenesis</h3>

1165<p id="_313" tabindex="-1">

1166Increased incidence of MCC has also been seen in people treated heavily with methoxsalen (psoralen) and ultraviolet A (PUVA) for psoriasis (3 of 1,380 patients, 0.2%). This has also been seen in individuals with chronic immune suppression, especially from chronic lymphocytic leukemia, HIV, and previous solid organ transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

116716</a>

1168,<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

116924</a>

1170]</p>

1171<p id="_254" tabindex="-1">

1172In 2008, a novel polyomavirus (Merkel cell polyoma virus [MCPyV]) was first reported in MCC tumor specimens [<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

117325</a>

1174], a finding subsequently confirmed in other laboratories.[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

117526</a>

1176-<a href="#cit/section_1.28">

117728</a>

1178] High levels of viral DNA and clonal integration of the virus in MCC tumors have also been reported [<a href="#cit/section_1.29">

117929</a>

1180] along with expression of certain viral antigens in MCC cells and the presence of antiviral antibodies. Not all cases of MCC appear to be associated with MCPyV infection.[<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

118130</a>

1182] </p>

1183<p id="_314" tabindex="-1">

1184MCPyV has been detected at very low levels in normal skin distant from the MCC primary, in a significant percentage of patients with non-MCC cutaneous disorders, in normal-appearing skin in healthy individuals, and in nonmelanoma skin cancers in immune-suppressed individuals.[<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

118510</a>

1186,<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

118731</a>

1188-<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

118933</a>

1190] Various methods have been used to identify and quantify the presence of MCPyV in MCC tumor specimens, other non-MCC tumors, blood, urine, and other tissues.[<a href="#cit/section_1.34">

119134</a>

1192,<a href="#cit/section_1.35">

119335</a>

1194]</p>

1195<p id="_315" tabindex="-1">

1196The significance of the new MCPyV findings remains uncertain. The prognostic significance of viral load, antibody titer levels, and the role of underlying immunosuppression in hosts (from disease and medications) are under investigation. </p>

1197<p id="_316" tabindex="-1">

1198Prevalence of MCPyV appears to differ between MCC patients in the United States and Europe versus Australia. There may be two independent pathways for the development of MCC: one driven by the presence of MCPyV, and the other driven primarily by sun damage, especially as noted in patient series from Australia.[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

119926</a>

1200,<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

120130</a>

1202,<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

120336</a>

1204] </p>

1205<p id="_255" tabindex="-1">

1206Although no unique marker for MCC has been identified, a variety of molecular and cytogenetic markers of MCC have been reported.[<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

12077</a>

1208,<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

120910</a>

1210,<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

121117</a>

1212]</p>

1213</section>

1214<section id="_256">

1215<h3 id="_256_toc">

1216Clinical Presentation</h3>

1217<p id="_257" tabindex="-1">

1218MCC usually presents as a painless, indurated, solitary dermal nodule with a slightly erythematous to deeply violaceous color, and rarely, an ulcer. MCC can infiltrate locally via dermal lymphatics, resulting in multiple satellite lesions. Because of its nonspecific clinical appearance, MCC is rarely suspected before a biopsy is performed.[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

12195</a>

1220] Photographs of MCC skin lesions illustrate its clinical variability.[<a href="#cit/section_1.37">

122137</a>

1222]</p>

1223<p id="_258" tabindex="-1">

1224A mnemonic [<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

122519</a>

1226] summarizing typical clinical characteristics of MCC has been proposed: </p>

1227<div class="pdq-content-list">

1228<p class="pdq-list-title">

1229AEIOU</p>

1230<ul id="_259">

1231<li>

1232A = Asymptomatic.</li>

1233<li>

1234E = Expanding rapidly.</li>

1235<li>

1236I = Immune suppressed.</li>

1237<li>

1238O = Older than 50 years.</li>

1239<li>

1240U = UV-exposed skin.</li>

1241</ul>

1242</div>

1243<p id="_260" tabindex="-1">

1244Not all patients have every element in this mnemonic; however, in this study, 89% of patients met three or more criteria, 52% met four or more criteria, and 7% met all five criteria.[<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

124519</a>

1246]</p>

1247</section>

1248<section id="_261">

1249<h3 id="_261_toc">

1250Initial Clinical Evaluation</h3>

1251<p id="_262" tabindex="-1">

1252Because local-regional spread is common, newly diagnosed MCC patients require a careful clinical examination that includes looking for satellite lesions and regional nodal involvement. </p>

1253<p id="_263" tabindex="-1">

1254Tailoring an imaging work-up to the clinical presentation and any relevant signs and symptoms should be considered. There has been no systematic study of the optimal imaging work-up for newly diagnosed patients, and it is not clear if all newly diagnosed patients, especially those with the smallest primaries, benefit from a detailed imaging work-up. </p>

1255<p id="_264" tabindex="-1">

1256If an imaging work-up is performed, it may include a computed tomography (CT) scan of the chest and abdomen to rule out primary small cell lung cancer as well as distant and regional metastases. Imaging studies designed to evaluate suspicious signs and symptoms may also be recommended. In one series, CT scans had an 80% false-negative rate for regional metastases.[<a href="#cit/section_1.38">

125738</a>

1258] Head and neck presentations may require additional imaging. Magnetic resonance imaging has been used to evaluate MCC but has not been studied systematically.[<a href="#cit/section_1.39">

125939</a>

1260] Fluorine F 18-fludeoxyglucose positron emission tomography results have been reported in selected cases.[<a href="#cit/section_1.40">

126140</a>

1262,<a href="#cit/section_1.41">

126341</a>

1264] Baseline routine blood work has been recommended but has not been studied systematically. There are no known circulating tumor markers specifically for MCC.</p>

1265</section>

1266<section id="_265">

1267<h3 id="_265_toc">

1268Initial Staging Results</h3>

1269<p id="_266" tabindex="-1">

1270The results of initial clinical staging of MCC vary widely in the literature, based on retrospective case series reported over decades. For invasive cancers, 48.6% were localized, 31.1% were regional, and 8.2% were distant.[<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

127118</a>

1272]</p>

1273<p id="_267" tabindex="-1">

1274MCC that presents in regional nodes without an identifiable primary lesion is found in a minority of patients, with the percent of these cases varying among the reported series. Tumors without an identifiable primary lesion have been attributed to either spontaneous regression of the primary or metastatic neuroendocrine carcinoma from a clinically occult site.[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

12758</a>

1276,<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

127718</a>

1278,<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

127919</a>

1280,<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

128142</a>

1282,<a href="#cit/section_1.43">

128343</a>

1284]</p>

1285</section>

1286<section id="_268">

1287<h3 id="_268_toc">

1288Clinical Progression</h3>

1289<p id="_269" tabindex="-1">

1290In a review of patients from 18 case series, 279 of 926 patients (30.1%) developed local recurrence during follow-up, excluding those presenting with distant metastatic disease. These events have been typically attributed to inadequate surgical margins and/or a lack of adjuvant radiation therapy. In addition, 545 of 982 patients (55.5%) had lymph node metastases at diagnosis or during follow-up.[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

12918</a>

1292]</p>

1293<p id="_270" tabindex="-1">

1294In the same review of 18 case series, the most common sites of distant metastases were distant lymph nodes (60.1%), distant skin (30.3%), lung (23.4%), central nervous system (18.4%), and bone (15.2%).[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

12958</a>

1296] Many other sites of disease have also been reported, and the distribution of metastatic sites varies among case series. </p>

1297<p id="_271" tabindex="-1">

1298In one series of 237 patients presenting with local or regional disease, the median time-to-recurrence was 9 months (range, 2&#8211;70 months). Ninety-one percent of recurrences occurred within 2 years of diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.44">

129944</a>

1300]</p>

1301</section>

1302<section id="_272">

1303<h3 id="_272_toc">

1304Potential Prognostic Factors</h3>

1305<p id="_273" tabindex="-1">

1306The extent of disease at presentation may provide the most useful estimate of prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

13077</a>

1308]</p>

1309<p id="_274" tabindex="-1">

1310Diagnostic procedures, such as sentinel lymph node biopsy, may help distinguish between local and regional disease at presentation. One-third of patients who lack clinically palpable or radiologically visible nodes will have microscopically evident regional disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.38">

131138</a>

1312] Nodal positivity may be substantially lower among patients with small tumors (e.g., &#8804;1.0 cm).[<a href="#cit/section_1.45">

131345</a>

1314]</p>

1315<p id="_275" tabindex="-1">

1316Many retrospective studies have evaluated the relationship of a wide variety of biological and histological factors to survival and local-regional control.[<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

13177</a>

1318,<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

13198</a>

1320,<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

132118</a>

1322,<a href="#cit/section_1.38">

132338</a>

1324,<a href="#cit/section_1.44">

132544</a>

1326,<a href="#cit/section_1.46">

132746</a>

1328-<a href="#cit/section_1.57">

132957</a>

1330][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1331Level of evidence C2</a>

1332] Many of these reports are confounded by small numbers, potential selection bias, referral bias, short follow-up, no uniform clinical protocol for both staging and treatment, and are underpowered to detect modest differences.</p>

1333<p id="_276" tabindex="-1">

1334A large, single-institution, retrospective study of 156 MCC patients, with a median follow-up of 51 months (range, 2&#8211;224 months), evaluated histological factors potentially associated with prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.55">

133555</a>

1336][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1337Level of evidence C1</a>

1338] Although this report was subject to potential selection and referral bias, both univariate and multivariate analyses demonstrated a relationship between improved cause-specific survival and circumscribed growth pattern versus infiltrative pattern, shallow-tumor depth versus deep-tumor depth, and absence of lymphovascular invasion versus presence of lymphovascular invasion. Adoption of these findings into a global prognostic algorithm awaits independent confirmation by adequately powered studies. </p>

1339<p id="_277" tabindex="-1">

1340A 2009 study investigated whether the presence of newly identified MCPyV in MCC tumor specimens influenced clinical outcome among 114 Finnish patients with MCC. In this small study, patients whose tumors were MCPyV-positive appeared to have better survival than patients whose tumors were MCPyV-negative.[<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

134158</a>

1342][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1343Level of evidence C2</a>

1344] Standardization of procedures to identify and quantify MCPyV and relevant antibodies is needed to improve understanding of both prognostic and epidemiological questions.[<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

134510</a>

1346] </p>

1347</section>

1348<section id="_278">

1349<h3 id="_278_toc">

1350Prognosis</h3>

1351<p id="_397" tabindex="-1">

1352The most significant prognostic parameters for MCC include tumor size and the presence of locoregional or distant metastases. These factors form the basis of the American Joint Committee on Cancer staging system for MCC.[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

135359</a>

1354,<a href="#cit/section_1.60">

135560</a>

1356] Although an increasing primary tumor size correlates with an increased risk of metastatic disease, MCC tumors of any size have significant risk of occult metastasis, supporting the use of sentinel lymph node biopsy for all cases.[<a href="#cit/section_1.61">

135761</a>

1358] Additional features of the primary tumor, such as lymphovascular invasion and tumor growth pattern, may also have prognostic significance. Clinically detectable nodal disease is associated with worse outcome than microscopic metastases.[<a href="#cit/section_1.55">

135955</a>

1360,<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

136159</a>

1362] Other findings associated with worse prognosis include sheet-like involvement in lymph node metastases and an increasing number of metastatic lymph nodes.[<a href="#cit/section_1.60">

136360</a>

1364,<a href="#cit/section_1.62">

136562</a>

1366]</p>

1367<p id="_279" tabindex="-1">

1368The bulk of MCC literature is from small case series, which are subject to many confounding factors. For this reason, the relapse and survival rates reported by stage vary widely in the literature. In general, lower-stage disease is associated with better overall survival.[<a href="#cit/section_1.63">

136963</a>

1370] For more information, see the <a href="/types/skin/hp/merkel-cell-treatment-pdq#_272">

1371Potential Prognostic Factors</a>

1372 section.</p>

1373<p id="_280" tabindex="-1">

1374Outcomes from patients presenting with small volume local disease and pathologically confirmed cancer-negative lymph nodes report a cause-specific 5-year survival rate exceeding 90% in one report.[<a href="#cit/section_1.44">

137544</a>

1376,<a href="#cit/section_1.55">

137755</a>

1378][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1379Level of evidence C2</a>

1380]</p>

1381<p id="_281" tabindex="-1">

1382A tabular summary of treatment results of MCC from 12 series illustrates the difficulty in comparing outcome data among series.[<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

13837</a>

1384]</p>

1385<p id="_282" tabindex="-1">

1386Using the SEER Program registry MCC staging system adopted in 1973, MCC survival data (1973&#8211;2006) by stage is summarized below:[<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

138718</a>

1388]<figure id="figure_220" class="image-center">

1389<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/658578.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1390Enlarge</a>

1391<img id="_220" alt="MerkelCell" title="MerkelCell" src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/658578-750.jpg">

1392<figcaption class="caption-container">

1393Figure 3. Relative ten-year survival rates for Merkel Cell Carcinoma by stage (SEER 1973&#8211;2006). Albores-Saavedra J et al: Merkel cell carcinoma demographics, morphology, and survival based on 3,870 cases: A population-based study. J Cutan Pathol. Reprinted with permission &#169; 2009. Published by Wiley-Blackwell. All rights reserved.</figcaption>

1394</figure>

1395</p>

1396</section>

1651

1652

1653 <section id="_36">

1654

1655 <h2>

1656Cellular Classification of Merkel Cell Carcinoma</h2>

1657

1658 <div id="_section_36" class="pdq-sections">

1659<p id="_37" tabindex="-1">

1660Although the exact origin and function of the Merkel cell remains under investigation, it is thought to have features of both epithelial and neuroendocrine origin and arise in cells with touch-sensitivity function (mechanoreceptors).[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

16611</a>

1662-<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

16634</a>

1664] </p>

1665<p id="_167" tabindex="-1">

1666Characteristic histopathological features include dense core cytoplasmic neurosecretory granules on electron microscopy and cytokeratin-20 on immunohistochemistry (see <a href="/types/skin/hp/merkel-cell-treatment-pdq#_241">

1667Figure 4</a>

1668).[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

16695</a>

1670] </p>

1671<p id="_168" tabindex="-1">

1672A panel of immunoreagents (see <a href="/types/skin/hp/merkel-cell-treatment-pdq#_241">

1673Figure 4</a>

1674) can distinguish between Merkel cell carcinoma (MCC) and other similar-appearing tumors including neuroendocrine carcinoma of the lung (i.e., small cell carcinoma), lymphoma, peripheral primitive neuroectodermal tumor, metastatic carcinoid tumor, and small cell melanoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

16755</a>

1676]<figure id="figure_241" class="image-center">

1677<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/661439.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1678Enlarge</a>

1679<img id="_241" alt="MerkelCell" title="MerkelCell" src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/661439-750.jpg">

1680<figcaption class="caption-container">

1681Figure 4. Merkel - Immunohistochemical differential diagnosis of Merkel-Cell Carcinoma (Typical Staining Pattern).</figcaption>

1682</figure>

1683</p>

1684<p id="_39" tabindex="-1">

1685Histologically, MCC has been classified into three distinct subtypes: [<a href="#cit/section_2.6">

16866</a>

1687-<a href="#cit/section_2.9">

16889</a>

1689]</p>

1690<div class="pdq-content-list">

1691<ul id="_40">

1692<li>

1693Trabecular: classic pattern, large-cell type, high density or granules on ultrasound examination.</li>

1694<li>

1695 Intermediate: solid pattern (most common).</li>

1696<li>

1697 Small cell: diffuse, few high-density granules on ultrasound examination (second most common).</li>

1698</ul>

1699</div>

1700<p id="_41" tabindex="-1">

1701Mixtures of variants are common.[<a href="#cit/section_2.6">

17026</a>

1703-<a href="#cit/section_2.8">

17048</a>

1705] Although some small retrospective case series have suggested correlations between certain histological features and outcome, the evidence remains uncertain.[<a href="#cit/section_2.10">

170610</a>

1707-<a href="#cit/section_2.12">

170812</a>

1709]</p>

1710<p id="_42" tabindex="-1">

1711One group has suggested a list of 12 elements that should be described in pathology reports of resected primary lesions and nine elements to be described in pathology reports of sentinel lymph nodes. The prognostic significance of these elements has not been validated prospectively.[<a href="#cit/section_2.13">

171213</a>

1713]</p>

1714<p id="_171" tabindex="-1">

1715If the following data are recorded for every MCC patient, any patient can be staged with the existing or new staging system:</p>

1716<div class="pdq-content-list">

1717<ul id="_172">

1718<li>

1719Size of primary tumor (maximum dimension pathologically or clinically in centimeters).</li>

1720<li>

1721Presence/absence of primary tumor invasion into bone, muscle, fascia, or cartilage.</li>

1722<li>

1723Presence/absence of nodal metastasis.</li>

1724<li>

1725Method used to ascertain status of nodal involvement (clinical or pathological examination).</li>

1726<li>

1727Presence/absence of distant metastasis. </li>

1728</ul>

1729</div>

1730<p id="_317" tabindex="-1">

1731The College of American Pathologists has published a protocol for the examination of specimens from patients with MCC of the skin.[<a href="#cit/section_2.14">

173214</a>

1733]</p>

1734<p id="_196" tabindex="-1">

1735For more information, see the <a href="/types/skin/hp/merkel-cell-treatment-pdq#_45">

1736Stage Information for Merkel Cell Carcinoma </a>

1737 section.</p>

1738<p id="_232" tabindex="-1">

1739The histological variants of MCC are shown in <a href="/types/skin/hp/merkel-cell-treatment-pdq#_221">

1740Figure 5.</a>

1741&#160;[<a href="#cit/section_2.15">

174215</a>

1743]<figure id="figure_221" class="image-center">

1744<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/658577.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1745Enlarge</a>

1746<img id="_221" alt="MerkelCell" title="MerkelCell" src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/658577-750.jpg">

1747<figcaption class="caption-container">

1748Figure 5. (A) Intermediate variant of MCC showing vesicular, basophilic nuclei with prominent nucleoli and multiple mitoses. (B) Small-cell variant, histologically indistinguishable from bronchial small-cell carcinoma. &#169;) Trabecular variant is rare and normally only seen as a small component of a mixed variant. Goessling W et al: Merkel Cell Carcinoma, J Clin Oncol, 20 (2), pp. 588&#8211;98. Reprinted with permission. &#169; 2009 American Society of Clinical Oncology. All rights reserved.</figcaption>

1749</figure>

1750</p>

1820Stage Information for Merkel Cell Carcinoma</h2>

1830</li>

1831</ul>

1832</nav>

1960<p id="_177" tabindex="-1">

1961These staging systems are highly inconsistent with each other. Stage III disease can mean anything from advanced local disease to nodal disease to distant metastatic disease. Furthermore, all MCC staging systems in use have been based on fewer than 300 patients. </p>

1962<section id="_285">

1963<h3 id="_285_toc">

1964American Joint Committee on Cancer (AJCC) Stage Groupings and TNM Definitions</h3>

1965<p id="_178" tabindex="-1">

1966To address these concerns, a new MCC-specific consensus staging system was developed by the AJCC to define MCC.[<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

19677</a>

1968] Before the publication of this new system, the AJCC advocated using the nonmelanoma staging system.</p>

1969<p id="_358" tabindex="-1">

1970Cancers staged using this staging system include primary cutaneous neuroendocrine carcinoma (MCC).</p>

1971<section id="_384">

1972<p id="_385" tabindex="-1">

2766<p id="_179" tabindex="-1">

2767Before the new AJCC consensus staging system was published, the most recent Memorial Sloan Kettering Cancer Center (MSKCC) four-stage system was favored because it was based on the largest number of patients and was the best validated.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

27681</a>

2769] The stages in the MSKCC system included: </p>

2770<div class="pdq-content-list">

2771<ul id="_47">

2772<li>

2773Stage I: local disease &lt;2 cm.

2774

2775</li>

2776<li>

2777Stage II: local disease &#8805;2 cm.</li>

2778<li>

2779Stage III: regional nodal disease.

2780</li>

2781<li>

2782Stage IV: distant metastatic disease.

2783</li>

2784</ul>

2785</div>

2786<p id="_195" tabindex="-1">

2787One group has suggested a list of 12 elements that should be described in pathology reports of resected primary lesions and nine elements to be described in pathology reports of sentinel lymph nodes. The prognostic significance of these elements has not been validated prospectively.[<a href="#cit/section_3.8">

27888</a>

2789] The 2009 AJCC staging manual also specifies a variety of factors that should be collected prospectively on pathology reports.</p>

2827

2828 <section id="_49">

2829

2830 <h2>

2831Treatment Option Overview</h2>

2832

2833 <div id="_section_49" class="pdq-sections">

2834<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2835<h6>

2836In This Section</h6>

2837<ul>

2838<li>

2839<a href="#_52_toc">

2840Surgery for the Primary Lesion</a>

2841</li>

2842<li>

2843<a href="#_55_toc">

2844Regional Lymph Node Surgery</a>

2845</li>

2846<li>

2847<a href="#_62_toc">

2848Radiation Therapy</a>

2849</li>

2850<li>

2851<a href="#_398_toc">

2852Immunotherapy </a>

2853</li>

2854<li>

2855<a href="#_67_toc">

2856Chemotherapy</a>

2857</li>

2858<li>

2859<a href="#_71_toc">

2860Follow-up</a>

2861</li>

2862<li>

2863<a href="#_TrialSearch_49_sid_4_toc">

2864Current Clinical Trials</a>

2865</li>

2866</ul>

2867</nav>

2868<p id="_51" tabindex="-1">

2869Merkel cell carcinoma (MCC) is an uncommon tumor. Most clinical management recommendations in the literature are based on case series that describe a relatively small number of patients who were not entered on formal clinical trials, evaluated with uniform clinical staging procedures, treated with uniform treatment protocols, or provided with regular, prescribed follow-up. These reports are also confounded by potential selection bias, referral bias, and short follow-up. They are underpowered to detect modest differences in outcome. </p>

2870<p id="_206" tabindex="-1">

2871In addition, outcomes of patients with American Joint Committee on Cancer stage I and stage II disease are often reported together. In the absence of results from clinical trials with prescribed work-up, treatments, and follow-up, most MCC patients have been treated using institutional or practitioner preferences that consider the specifics of each case as well as patient preference.</p>

2872<p id="_207" tabindex="-1">

2873There are two competing philosophies about the most appropriate method of treating MCC. In the first philosophy, MCC is treated like other nonmelanoma skin cancers, with an emphasis on treating local-regional disease with surgery and radiation therapy, as appropriate. In the second philosophy, MCC is treated according to its biological features. This approach makes it analogous to small cell lung cancer, which is assumed to be a systemic disease, and leads to a more routine recommendation of systematic adjuvant chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

28741</a>

2875]</p>

2876<section id="_52">

2877<h3 id="_52_toc">

2878Surgery for the Primary Lesion</h3>

2879<p id="_53" tabindex="-1">

2880In a review of 18 case series, 279 of 926 patients (30.1%) developed local recurrence during follow-up, excluding those presenting with distant metastatic disease at presentation. These recurrences have been typically attributed to inadequate surgical margins or possibly a lack of adjuvant radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

28812</a>

2882,<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

28833</a>

2884]

2885</p>

2886<p id="_182" tabindex="-1">

2887Given the propensity of MCC to recur locally (sometimes with satellite lesions and/or in-transit metastases), wide local excision to reduce the risk of local recurrence has been recommended for patients with clinical stage I or stage II disease. </p>

2888<p id="_183" tabindex="-1">

2889Recommendations about the optimal minimum width and depth of normal tissue margin to be excised around the primary tumor differ among the various retrospective case series, but this question has not been studied systematically.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

28903</a>

2891-<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

28927</a>

2893][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2894Level of evidence C2</a>

2895] No definitive data suggest that extremely wide margins improve overall survival (OS), although some reports suggest that wider margins appear to improve local control.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

28963</a>

2897][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2898Level of evidence C2</a>

2899] Frozen-section evaluation of margins may be useful, especially when the tumor is in an anatomical site that is not amenable to wide margins.

2900</p>

2901<p id="_54" tabindex="-1">

2902Some authors have advocated the use of <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=442898&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=Cancer.gov" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000442898&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=Cancer.gov'); return(false);">

2903Mohs micrographic surgery</a>

2904 as a tissue-sparing technique. The relapse rate has been reported to be similar to or better than that of wide excision, but comparatively few cases have been treated in this manner and none in randomized controlled trials.[<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

29057</a>

2906-<a href="#cit/section_4.10">

290710</a>

2908][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2909Level of evidence C2</a>

2910]</p>

2911</section>

2912<section id="_55">

2913<h3 id="_55_toc">

2914Regional Lymph Node Surgery</h3>

2915<p id="_56" tabindex="-1">

2916In some case series, local-regional recurrence rates are high when pathological nodal staging is omitted. Surgical nodal staging in clinically negative patients has identified positive nodes in at least 25% to 35% of patients.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

29174</a>

2918,<a href="#cit/section_4.11">

291911</a>

2920,<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

292112</a>

2922][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2923Level of evidence C2</a>

2924] In one retrospective series of 213 patients who underwent surgical treatment of the primary tumor and evaluation of the draining nodes, nodal positivity was found in 2 of 54 patients with small tumors (e.g., &#8804;1.0 cm) and 51 of 159 patients with tumors larger than 1.0 cm.[<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

292513</a>

2926][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2927Level of evidence C2</a>

2928]</p>

2929<p id="_57" tabindex="-1">

2930The role of elective lymph node dissection (ELND) in the absence of clinically positive lymph nodes has not been studied in formal clinical trials. In small case series, ELND has been recommended for larger primary tumors, tumors with more than ten mitoses per high-power field, lymphatic or vascular invasion, and the small-cell histological subtypes.[<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

293114</a>

2932-<a href="#cit/section_4.16">

293316</a>

2934][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2935Level of evidence C2</a>

2936]</p>

2937<p id="_58" tabindex="-1">

2938Sentinel lymph node (SLN) biopsy has been suggested as a preferred initial alternative to complete ELND for the proper staging of MCC. SLN biopsy has less morbidity than complete nodal dissection. Furthermore, for MCC sites with indeterminate lymphatic drainage, such as those on the back, SLN biopsy techniques can be used to identify the pertinent lymph node bed(s). If performed, SLN biopsy is done at the time of the wide resection when the local lymphatic channels are still intact. </p>

2939<p id="_59" tabindex="-1">

2940Several reports have found the use of SLN biopsy techniques in MCC to be reliable and reproducible.[<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

294117</a>

2942-<a href="#cit/section_4.20">

294320</a>

2944] However, the significance of SLN positivity remains unclear.</p>

2945<div class="pdq-content-list">

2946<ul id="_318">

2947<li>

2948One meta-analysis of ten case series found that SLN positivity strongly predicted a high short-term risk of recurrence and that subsequent therapeutic lymph node dissection was effective in preventing short-term regional nodal recurrence.[<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

294921</a>

2950] </li>

2951<li>

2952Another meta-analysis of 12 retrospective case series (only partially overlapping the collection of case series in the previous meta-analysis) found that:[<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

295312</a>

2954][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2955Level of evidence C2</a>

2956]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2957<ul id="_319" class="list-dash">

2958<li>

2959SLN biopsy detected MCC spread in one-third of patients whose tumors would have otherwise been clinically and radiologically understaged.</li>

2960<li>

2961The recurrence rate was three times higher in patients with a positive SLN biopsy than in those with a negative SLN biopsy (<em>

2962P</em>

2963 = .03).</li>

2964</ul>

2965</div>

2966</li>

2967<li>

2968Between 2006 and 2010, a large, retrospective, single-institutional series of 95 patients (with a total of 97 primary tumors) identified a SLN in 93 instances, and nodal tumor was seen in 42 patients. Immunohistochemical techniques were used to assess node positivity. Various models of tumor and patient characteristics were studied to predict node positivity. There was no subgroup of patients predicted to have lower than 15% to 20% likelihood of SLN positivity, suggesting that SLN biopsy may be considered for all curative patients with clinically negative lymph nodes and no distant metastases.[<a href="#cit/section_4.22">

296922</a>

2970][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2971Level of evidence C2</a>

2972]</li>

2973<li>

2974From 1996 to 2010, another retrospective, single-institutional study of 153 patients with localized MCC who underwent SLN biopsy analyzed factors associated with SLN positivity. The best predictors of SLN biopsy positivity were tumor size and lymphovascular invasion.[<a href="#cit/section_4.22">

297522</a>

2976,<a href="#cit/section_4.23">

297723</a>

2978][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2979Level of evidence C2</a>

2980]</li>

2981</ul>

2982</div>

2983<p id="_60" tabindex="-1">

2984In the absence of adequately powered, prospective, randomized clinical trials, the following questions remain:[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

29854</a>

2986,<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

298712</a>

2988,<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

298921</a>

2990,<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

299124</a>

2992][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2993Level of evidence C2</a>

2994]</p>

2995<div class="pdq-content-list">

2996<ul id="_99">

2997<li>

2998 Should every positive SLN biopsy be followed routinely by completion nodal surgery and/or radiation therapy? </li>

2999<li>

3000Are outcomes demonstrably improved by routinely adding radiation if lymph node surgery reveals tumor in multiple nodes and/or extracapsular extension and/or lymphovascular invasion? </li>

3001<li>

3002 Should patients with MCCs smaller than 1 cm routinely undergo sentinel lymph node dissection (SLND)? </li>

3003<li>

3004 Should patients with negative or omitted nodal work-up routinely undergo local or local-regional radiation therapy?</li>

3005<li>

3006Should immunohistochemical staining techniques be used to identify micrometastases in lymph nodes, and is micrometastatic disease in nodes clinically relevant?</li>

3007</ul>

3008</div>

3009<p id="_189" tabindex="-1">

3010Lymph node surgery is primarily used for staging and guiding additional treatment.</p>

3011<p id="_61" tabindex="-1">

3012 Based on a small number of retrospective studies, therapeutic dissection of the regional lymph nodes after a positive SLND appears to minimize, but not totally eliminate, the risk of subsequent regional lymph node recurrence and in-transit metastases.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

30134</a>

3014,<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

301521</a>

3016,<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

301724</a>

3018][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3019Level of evidence C2</a>

3020] There are no data from prospective randomized trials demonstrating that definitive regional nodal treatment with surgery improves survival.</p>

3021</section>

3022<section id="_62">

3023<h3 id="_62_toc">

3024Radiation Therapy</h3>

3025<p id="_63" tabindex="-1">

3026Because of the aggressive nature of MCC, its apparent radiosensitivity, and the high incidence of local and regional recurrences (including in-transit metastases after surgery alone to the primary tumor bed), some clinicians have recommended adjuvant radiation therapy to the primary site and nodal basin. Nodal basin radiation in contiguity with radiation to the primary site has been considered, especially for patients with larger tumors, locally unresectable tumors, close or positive excision margins that cannot be improved by additional surgery, and those with positive regional lymph nodes, especially after SLND (stage II).[<a href="#cit/section_4.10">

302710</a>

3028,<a href="#cit/section_4.11">

302911</a>

3030,<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

303114</a>

3032,<a href="#cit/section_4.15">

303315</a>

3034,<a href="#cit/section_4.25">

303525</a>

3036][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3037Level of evidence C2</a>

3038] Several small retrospective series have shown that radiation therapy plus adequate surgery improves local-regional control compared with surgery alone, [<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

30392</a>

3040,<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

30415</a>

3042,<a href="#cit/section_4.26">

304326</a>

3044-<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

304529</a>

3046] whereas other series did not show the same results.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

30474</a>

3048,<a href="#cit/section_4.8">

30498</a>

3050][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3051Level of evidence C2</a>

3052] </p>

3053<p id="_64" tabindex="-1">

3054In the absence of adequately powered, prospective, randomized clinical trials, the following questions remain:[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

30554</a>

3056,<a href="#cit/section_4.8">

30578</a>

3058,<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

30599</a>

3060,<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

306112</a>

3062,<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

306321</a>

3064,<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

306524</a>

3066,<a href="#cit/section_4.26">

306726</a>

3068,<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

306930</a>

3070-<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

307134</a>

3072][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3073Level of evidence C2</a>

3074] </p>

3075<div class="pdq-content-list">

3076<ul id="_100">

3077<li>

3078Should every positive SLN biopsy be followed routinely by completion nodal surgery and/or radiation therapy?</li>

3079<li>

3080 Are outcomes demonstrably improved by routinely adding radiation only if nodal surgery reveals tumor in multiple lymph nodes and/or extracapsular extension and/or lymphovascular invasion?</li>

3081<li>

3082 Should all or just certain patients with negative or omitted nodal work-up receive local or local-regional radiation routinely?</li>

3083</ul>

3084</div>

3085<p id="_101" tabindex="-1">

3086Because of the small size of these nonrandomized, retrospective series, the precise benefit from radiation therapy remains unproven.</p>

3087<p id="_65" tabindex="-1">

3088When recommended, the radiation dose given has been at least 50 Gy to the surgical bed with margins and to the draining regional lymphatics, delivered in 2 Gy fractions. For patients with unresected tumors or tumors with microscopic evidence of spread beyond resected margins, higher doses of 56 Gy to 65 Gy to the primary site have been recommended.[<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

30895</a>

3090,<a href="#cit/section_4.10">

309110</a>

3092,<a href="#cit/section_4.11">

309311</a>

3094,<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

309514</a>

3096,<a href="#cit/section_4.15">

309715</a>

3098,<a href="#cit/section_4.27">

309927</a>

3100,<a href="#cit/section_4.31">

310131</a>

3102,<a href="#cit/section_4.35">

310335</a>

3104][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3105Level of evidence C2</a>

3106] These doses have not been studied prospectively in clinical trials. </p>

3107<p id="_320" tabindex="-1">

3108Local and/or regional control of MCC with radiation therapy alone has been reported in small, highly selected, nonrandomized case series of patients with diverse clinical characteristics.[<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

310929</a>

3110,<a href="#cit/section_4.36">

311136</a>

3112] Typically, these patients have had inoperable primary tumors and/or nodes or were considered medically inappropriate for surgery.[<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

311329</a>

3114,<a href="#cit/section_4.36">

311536</a>

3116][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3117Level of evidence C2</a>

3118]</p>

3119<p id="_66" tabindex="-1">

3120Retrospective Surveillance, Epidemiology, and End Results (SEER) Program data suggest that adding radiation therapy to surgery adds survival value, but the conclusions are complicated by incomplete patient data, no protocol for evaluation and treatment, and potential sampling bias.[<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

312132</a>

3122] Prospective randomized clinical trials are required to assess whether combining surgery with radiation therapy affects survival.[<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

312333</a>

3124,<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

312534</a>

3126][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3127Level of evidence C2</a>

3128] </p>

3129</section>

3130<section id="_398">

3131<h3 id="_398_toc">

3132Immunotherapy </h3>

3133<p id="_399" tabindex="-1">

3134Approximately 70% to 80% of MCC cases in the United States are caused by Merkel cell polyomavirus (MCPyV), and within virus-positive MCCs, the viral oncoproteins (T antigens) are constitutively expressed and growth-promoting. Furthermore, patients with stimulated immune response to MCPyV have better disease outcomes, providing a rationale for the use of immunotherapy. </p>

3135<p id="_401" tabindex="-1">

3136In a phase II trial, 88 patients with chemotherapy-treated metastatic MCC received avelumab, a human anti&#8211;programmed death ligand-1 (PD-L1) monoclonal antibody (10 mg/kg intravenously [IV] every 2 weeks). The objective rate of response was 33%, and 11% of the patients had a complete response. Median response was at 6.1 weeks and was irrespective of MCPyV or PD-L1 status. More importantly, responses were durable, with 74% lasting at least 1 year. As a result, median OS was more than twice the historical median, that is, an OS of 4 to 6 months with second-line chemotherapy. Based on this study, the U.S. Food and Drug Administration approved avelumab in 2017 for the treatment of metastatic MCC, regardless of previous chemotherapy administration.[<a href="#cit/section_4.37">

313737</a>

3138-<a href="#cit/section_4.39">

313939</a>

3140]</p>

3141<p id="_400" tabindex="-1">

3142Pembrolizumab, a humanized IgG4 anti&#8211;programmed cell death-1 (PD-1) monoclonal antibody, is being studied in a phase II trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT02267603">

3143NCT02267603</a>

3144) of first-line systemic treatment for patients with unresectable stage IIIB or stage IV MCC. The initial report included 26 patients; 16% had a complete response and 40% had a partial response, resulting in an objective response rate of 56%. While the response rate was similar to historical rates with first-line chemotherapy, pembrolizumab responses were more durable, with 86% ongoing at last follow-up.[<a href="#cit/section_4.40">

314540</a>

3146]

3147

3148</p>

3149<p id="_402" tabindex="-1">

3150Nivolumab, another anti-PD-1 antibody, is being studied (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT02488759">

3151NCT02488759</a>

3152) in patients with virus-associated cancers, including MCC. Patients with metastatic MCC were eligible regardless of MCPyV status or previous chemotherapy. Preliminary results reported an ongoing response rate of 87%, with responses in 13 of 15 patients at last follow-up (median follow-up, 6 months). This trial added a second cohort to investigate nivolumab plus ipilimumab (1 mg/kg) in patients with metastatic MCC.[<a href="#cit/section_4.41">

315341</a>

3154]</p>

3155</section>

3156<section id="_67">

3157<h3 id="_67_toc">

3158Chemotherapy</h3>

3159<p id="_68" tabindex="-1">

3160A variety of chemotherapy regimens have been used for patients with MCC in the settings of adjuvant, advanced, and recurrent therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

31615</a>

3162,<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

316334</a>

3164,<a href="#cit/section_4.42">

316542</a>

3166,<a href="#cit/section_4.43">

316743</a>

3168] [<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3169Level of evidence C2</a>

3170] Even though no phase III clinical trials have been conducted to demonstrate that adjuvant chemotherapy produces improvements in OS, some clinicians recommend its use in most cases because of the following:</p>

3171<div class="pdq-content-list">

3172<ul id="_208">

3173<li>

3174A biologic analogy is made between MCC and the histologically similar small cell carcinoma of the lung, which is considered a systemic disease.</li>

3175<li>

3176The risk of metastases and progression with MCC is high.</li>

3177<li>

3178Good initial clinical response rates have been noted with some chemotherapy regimens.</li>

3179</ul>

3180</div>

3181<p id="_209" tabindex="-1">

3182 When possible, patients can be encouraged to participate in clinical trials.</p>

3183<p id="_69" tabindex="-1">

3184From 1997 to 2001, the Trans-Tasman Radiation Oncology Group performed a phase II evaluation of 53 MCC patients with high-risk, local-regional disease. High risk was defined as recurrence after initial therapy, involved lymph nodes, primary tumor larger than 1 cm, gross residual disease after surgery, or occult primary with positive lymph nodes. Therapy included local-regional radiation therapy (50 Gy in 25 fractions), synchronous carboplatin (area under the curve [AUC] 4.5), and IV etoposide (89 mg/m<span class="sup">

31852</span>

3186 on days 1&#8211;3 in weeks 1, 4, 7, and 10). Surgery was not standardized for either the primary tumor or the lymph nodes, and 12 patients had close margins, positive margins, or gross residual disease. Twenty-eight patients had undissected nodal beds, and the remainder had a variety of nodal surgeries. With a median follow-up of 48 months, the 3-year OS rate was 76%, the rate of local-regional control was 75%, and the rate of distant control was 76%. Radiation reactions in the skin and febrile neutropenia were significant clinical acute toxicities. Because of the heterogeneity of the population and the nonstandardized surgery, it is difficult to infer a clear treatment benefit from the chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.44">

318744</a>

3188][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3189Level of evidence C1</a>

3190] </p>

3191<p id="_102" tabindex="-1">

3192 In a subsequent report, the same investigators evaluated a subset of these protocol patients (n = 40, after excluding patients with unknown primaries) and compared them with 61 historical controls who received no chemotherapy, were treated at the same institutions, were diagnosed before 1997, and had no routine imaging staging studies. Radiation therapy was given to 50 patients. There was no significant survival benefit seen for chemotherapy patients.[<a href="#cit/section_4.45">

319345</a>

3194] </p>

3195<p id="_185" tabindex="-1">

3196In a subsequent pilot clinical trial of 18 patients from 2004 to 2006, the same investigators attempted to reduce the skin and hematological toxicity seen in Study 96-07. Carboplatin (AUC = 2) was administered weekly during radiation therapy beginning on day 1 for a maximum of five doses, followed by three cycles of carboplatin (AUC 4.5, and IV etoposide 80 mg/m<span class="sup">

31972</span>

3198 on days 1&#8211;3 beginning 3 weeks after radiation and repeated every 3 weeks for three cycles). The radiation therapy was similar to that in the earlier trial.[<a href="#cit/section_4.44">

319944</a>

3200] Results suggested a decrease in hematological and skin toxicity.[<a href="#cit/section_4.46">

320146</a>

3202]</p>

3203<p id="_70" tabindex="-1">

3204Use of chemotherapy has also been reported in selected patients with locally advanced and metastatic disease. In one retrospective study of 107 patients, 57% of patients with metastatic disease and 69% with locally advanced disease responded to initial chemotherapy. Median OS was 9 months for patients with metastatic disease and 24 months for patients with locally advanced disease. At 3 years, the OS rate was projected to be 17% for those with metastatic disease and 35% for those with locally advanced disease. Toxicity was significant, however, and without clear benefit, particularly in older patients.[<a href="#cit/section_4.47">

320547</a>

3206][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3207Level of evidence C2</a>

3208]</p>

3209</section>

3210<section id="_71">

3211<h3 id="_71_toc">

3212Follow-up</h3>

3213<p id="_72" tabindex="-1">

3214The most appropriate follow-up techniques and frequency for patients treated for MCC have not been prospectively studied. Because of the propensity for local and regional recurrence, clinicians should perform at least a thorough physical examination of the site of initial disease and the regional lymph nodes. Imaging studies may be ordered to evaluate signs and symptoms of concern, or they may be performed to identify distant metastases early; but, there are no data suggesting that early detection and treatment of new distant metastases results in improved survival. </p>

3215<p id="_194" tabindex="-1">

3216In one series of 237 patients presenting with local or regional disease, the median time-to-recurrence was 9 months (range, 2&#8211;70 months). Ninety-one percent of recurrences occurred within 2 years of diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

32174</a>

3218] It has been suggested that the intensity of follow-up can be gradually diminished after 2 to 3 years as the majority of recurrences are likely to have already occurred.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

32194</a>

3220]</p>

3221</section>

3224Current Clinical Trials</h3>

3225<p id="_TrialSearch_49_22" tabindex="-1">

3226Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

3227advanced clinical trial search</a>

3228 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

3229General information</a>

3230 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

3424 <section id="_73">

3425

3426 <h2>

3427Treatment of Stage I and II Merkel Cell Carcinoma</h2>

3428

3429 <div id="_section_73" class="pdq-sections">

3430<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

3431<h6>

3432In This Section</h6>

3433<ul>

3434<li>

3435<a href="#_409_toc">

3436Treatment Options for Stage I and II Merkel Cell Carcinoma</a>

3437</li>

3438<li>

3439<a href="#_TrialSearch_73_sid_5_toc">

3440Current Clinical Trials</a>

3441</li>

3442</ul>

3443</nav>

3444<p id="_186" tabindex="-1">

3445Stage I and II Merkel cell carcinoma (MCC) include patients with local disease only.</p>

3446<p id="_403" tabindex="-1">

3447Excision with 1 cm to 2 cm margins and radiation therapy are the mainstays of management for primary MCC tumors. Adjuvant radiation therapy to the primary tumor site is often recommended; however, the morbidity of radiation may be avoided and low local recurrence rates maintained, as shown in a subset of patients with small low-risk lesions (i.e., tumors &lt;2 cm without other adverse prognostic factors).[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

34481</a>

3449]</p>

3450<p id="_404" tabindex="-1">

3451Because of the risk of occult nodal disease, sentinel lymph node (SLN) biopsy is recommended for patients without clinically detectable metastatic disease.[<a href="#cit/section_5.2">

34522</a>

3453] Any size of metastatic deposit is currently considered positive with regard to regional lymph node (N) staging; therefore, immunohistochemistry is routinely used to improve detection of micrometastases in SLN.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

34543</a>

3455,<a href="#cit/section_5.4">

34564</a>

3457] </p>

3458<section id="_409">

3459<h3 id="_409_toc">

3460Treatment Options for Stage I and II Merkel Cell Carcinoma</h3>

3461<p id="_410" tabindex="-1">

3462Treatment options for <a href="/types/skin/hp/merkel-cell-treatment-pdq#_379">

3463stage I </a>

3464 and <a href="/types/skin/hp/merkel-cell-treatment-pdq#_380">

3465stage II MCC</a>

3466 include the following:</p>

3467<div class="pdq-content-list">

3468<ol id="_411">

3469<li>

3470Margin-negative local excision, attempting to maintain function. </li>

3471<li>

3472Surgical nodal evaluation, typically by SLN procedure initially, may be considered for patients with significant risk of nodal disease. Completion of the nodal dissection may be considered if positive lymph nodes are found, which would upstage the patient's cancer to stage III.</li>

3473<li>

3474Local radiation therapy may be considered if there is concern about the primary tumor excision margins. Regional radiation therapy may be considered if the nodal staging procedure is incomplete or omitted. For sites where the location of primary regional lymph nodes may be uncertain (e.g., mid-back), regional-node field selection is problematic.</li>

3475<li>

3476 Enrollment in clinical trials is encouraged.</li>

3477</ol>

3478</div>

3479</section>

3485advanced clinical trial search</a>

3486 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

3487General information</a>

3488 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

3516 <section id="_78">

3517

3518 <h2>

3519Treatment of Stage III Merkel Cell Carcinoma</h2>

3520

3521 <div id="_section_78" class="pdq-sections">

3522<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

3523<h6>

3524In This Section</h6>

3525<ul>

3526<li>

3527<a href="#_412_toc">

3528Treatment Options for Stage III Merkel Cell Carcinoma</a>

3529</li>

3530<li>

3531<a href="#_TrialSearch_78_sid_6_toc">

3532Current Clinical Trials</a>

3533</li>

3534</ul>

3535</nav>

3536<p id="_187" tabindex="-1">

3537Stage III Merkel cell carcinoma (MCC) includes patients with nodal disease.</p>

3538<section id="_412">

3539<h3 id="_412_toc">

3540Treatment Options for Stage III Merkel Cell Carcinoma</h3>

3541<p id="_413" tabindex="-1">

3542Treatment options for <a href="/types/skin/hp/merkel-cell-treatment-pdq#_382">

3543stage III MCC</a>

3544 include the following:</p>

3545<div class="pdq-content-list">

3546<ol id="_414">

3547<li>

3548Margin-negative local excision, attempting to maintain function.</li>

3549<li>

3550Sentinel lymph node procedure, possibly followed by more definitive regional node surgery if positive node(s) are found.</li>

3551<li>

3552Local and regional lymph node radiation, especially if there is concern about the adequacy of the primary tumor excision margin or the risk of local-regional recurrence following the nodal surgery (e.g., multiple primary nodes, extracapsular extension, lymphovascular invasion, and evidence of in-transit metastases).</li>

3553<li>

3554Systemic chemotherapy may be administered to patients with the highest risk of recurrence with the understanding that existing data have not proven a clinical survival benefit (under clinical evaluation). </li>

3555<li>

3556Enrollment in clinical trials is encouraged.</li>

3557</ol>

3558</div>

3559</section>

3566 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. 3576 <section id="_83">

3577

3578 <h2>

3579Treatment of Stage IV Merkel Cell Carcinoma</h2>

3580

3581 <div id="_section_83" class="pdq-sections">

3582<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

3583<h6>

3584In This Section</h6>

3585<ul>

3586<li>

3587<a href="#_415_toc">

3588Treatment Options for Stage IV Merkel Cell Carcinoma</a>

3589</li>

3590<li>

3591<a href="#_TrialSearch_83_sid_7_toc">

3592Current Clinical Trials</a>

3593</li>

3594</ul>

3595</nav>

3596<p id="_188" tabindex="-1">

3597Stage IV Merkel cell carcinoma (MCC) includes patients with distant metastases.</p>

3598<p id="_84" tabindex="-1">

3599Chemotherapy may be considered for patients with stage IV disease who have a good performance status. Although responses have been seen with various regimens, evidence is lacking that chemotherapy results in permanent disease control or long-term survival. </p>

3600<p id="_85" tabindex="-1">

3601In stage IV patients for whom chemotherapy is not considered an appropriate option, surgery and/or radiation therapy may be considered for local or regional palliation.</p>

3602<p id="_406" tabindex="-1">

3603Avelumab, an anti-programmed death ligand-1 antibody, has been approved by the U.S. Food and Drug Administration as therapy for metastatic MCC. Other immunotherapies for MCC are under clinical evaluation. The success of immune-based therapies represents a milestone in the management of advanced MCC. However, not all patients respond to immune-based therapies. Furthermore, patients requiring immunosuppression in the setting of solid organ transplants or those with autoimmune disease may not be optimal candidates for immune-based therapy. </p>

3604<section id="_415">

3605<h3 id="_415_toc">

3606Treatment Options for Stage IV Merkel Cell Carcinoma</h3>

3607<p id="_416" tabindex="-1">

3608Treatment options for <a href="/types/skin/hp/merkel-cell-treatment-pdq#_383">

3609stage IV MCC</a>

3610 include the following:</p>

3611<div class="pdq-content-list">

3612<ol id="_417">

3613<li>

3614Palliation with chemotherapy and/or surgery and/or radiation therapy as clinically appropriate.</li>

3615<li>

3616 Enrollment in clinical trials is strongly encouraged.</li>

3617</ol>

3618</div>

3619</section>

3625advanced clinical trial search</a>

3626 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

3627General information</a>

3628 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

3636 <section id="_90">

3637

3638 <h2>

3639Treatment of Recurrent Merkel Cell Carcinoma</h2>

3640

3641 <div id="_section_90" class="pdq-sections">

3642<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

3643<h6>

3644In This Section</h6>

3645<ul>

3646<li>

3647<a href="#_418_toc">

3648Treatment Options for Recurrent Merkel Cell Carcinoma<ul>

3649<li>

3650<a href="#_419_toc">

3651Local recurrence</a>

3652</li>

3653<li>

3654<a href="#_423_toc">

3655Nodal recurrence</a>

3656</li>

3657<li>

3658<a href="#_425_toc">

3659Distant recurrence</a>

3660</li>

3661</ul>

3662</a>

3663</li>

3664<li>

3665<a href="#_TrialSearch_90_sid_8_toc">

3666Current Clinical Trials</a>

3667</li>

3668</ul>

3669</nav>

3670<p id="_91" tabindex="-1">

3671Merkel cell carcinoma is a rare tumor. There are no clinical trials reported for patients with recurrent disease exclusively. Recommendations and outcomes of various treatments for these patients are included in many large case series [<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

36721</a>

3673-<a href="#cit/section_8.3">

36743</a>

3675][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3676Level of evidence C2</a>

3677] and one phase II clinical trial.[<a href="#cit/section_8.4">

36784</a>

3679][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3680Level of evidence C1</a>

3681] Treatments are usually individualized based on patient preference and the specifics of each case, and there are no standard options. Consideration should be given to enrollment in clinical trials.</p>

3682<section id="_418">

3683<h3 id="_418_toc">

3684Treatment Options for Recurrent Merkel Cell Carcinoma</h3>

3685<section id="_419">

3686<h4 id="_419_toc">

3687Local recurrence</h4>

3688<p id="_420" tabindex="-1">

3689Treatment options for patients with local recurrence include wider local surgery if possible, followed by radiation therapy, if not previously given.</p>

3690<p id="_421" tabindex="-1">

3691Regional lymph node dissection (RLND) can also be considered if regional draining nodes have not been previously removed.</p>

3692<p id="_422" tabindex="-1">

3693Because of the poor prognosis after recurrence, consideration can also be given to systemic chemotherapy, although there is no evidence that it improves survival.</p>

3694</section>

3695<section id="_423">

3696<h4 id="_423_toc">

3697Nodal recurrence</h4>

3698<p id="_424" tabindex="-1">

3699Treatment options for patients with only regional nodal recurrence include RLND and adjuvant radiation therapy if the regional draining nodes have not been previously treated. Because of the poor prognosis after recurrence, consideration can also be given to systemic chemotherapy, although there is no evidence that it improves survival.</p>

3700</section>

3701<section id="_425">

3702<h4 id="_425_toc">

3703Distant recurrence</h4>

3704<p id="_426" tabindex="-1">

3705For patients with distant recurrence only, chemotherapy is an option for patients who have good performance status.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

37061</a>

3707-<a href="#cit/section_8.6">

37086</a>

3709][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3710Level of evidence C2</a>

3711] Although responses with chemotherapy have been reported in selected patients with locally advanced and metastatic disease, toxicity has been significant and without clear benefit, particularly in older patients. When appropriate, radiation therapy and/or surgery may be offered as palliation to sites of recurrence, particularly if chemotherapy is not considered an option.</p>

3712</section>

3713</section>

3714<section id="_TrialSearch_90_sid_8">

3715<h3 id="_TrialSearch_90_sid_8_toc">

3716Current Clinical Trials</h3>

3717<p id="_TrialSearch_90_22" tabindex="-1">

3718Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

3719advanced clinical trial search</a>

3720 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

3721General information</a>

3722 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

3723</section>

3787 <section id="_AboutThis_1">

3788

3789 <h2>

3790About This PDQ Summary</h2>

3791

3792 <div id="_section_AboutThis_1" class="pdq-sections">

3793<section id="_AboutThis_2">

3794<h3 id="_AboutThis_2_toc">

3795Purpose of This Summary</h3>

3796<p id="_AboutThis_3" tabindex="-1">

3797This PDQ cancer information summary for health professionals provides comprehensive, peer-reviewed, evidence-based information about the treatment of merkel cell carcinoma. It is intended as a resource to inform and assist clinicians in the care of their patients. It does not provide formal guidelines or recommendations for making health care decisions.</p>

3798</section>

3799<section id="_AboutThis_4">

3800<h3 id="_AboutThis_4_toc">

3801Reviewers and Updates</h3>

3802<p id="_AboutThis_5" tabindex="-1">

3803This summary is reviewed regularly and updated as necessary by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/adult-treatment" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/adult-treatment">

3804PDQ Adult Treatment Editorial Board</a>

3805, which is editorially independent of the National Cancer Institute (NCI). The summary reflects an independent review of the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or the National Institutes of Health (NIH).</p>

3806<p id="_AboutThis_22" tabindex="-1">

3807 Board members review recently published articles each month to determine whether an article should:</p>

3808<div class="pdq-content-list">

3809<ul id="_AboutThis_6">

3810<li>

3811be discussed at a meeting,</li>

3812<li>

3813be cited with text, or</li>

3814<li>

3815replace or update an existing article that is already cited.</li>

3816</ul>

3817</div>

3818<p id="_AboutThis_7" tabindex="-1">

3819Changes to the summaries are made through a consensus process in which Board members evaluate the strength of the evidence in the published articles and determine how the article should be included in the summary.</p>

3820<p id="" tabindex="-1">

3821The lead reviewer for Merkel Cell Carcinoma Treatment is:</p>

3822<div class="pdq-content-list">

3823<ul id="">

3824<li>

3825Russell S. Berman, MD (New York University School of Medicine)</li>

3826</ul>

3827</div>

3828<p id="_AboutThis_9" tabindex="-1">

3829Any comments or questions about the summary content should be submitted to Cancer.gov through the NCI website's <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us" title="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us">

3830Email Us</a>

3831. Do not contact the individual Board Members with questions or comments about the summaries. Board members will not respond to individual inquiries.</p>

3832</section>

3833<section id="_AboutThis_10">

3834<h3 id="_AboutThis_10_toc">

3835Levels of Evidence</h3>

3836<p id="_AboutThis_11" tabindex="-1">

3837Some of the reference citations in this summary are accompanied by a level-of-evidence designation. These designations are intended to help readers assess the strength of the evidence supporting the use of specific interventions or approaches. The PDQ Adult Treatment Editorial Board uses a <a href="/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/treatment">

3838formal evidence ranking system</a>

3839 in developing its level-of-evidence designations.</p>

3840</section>

Although the content of PDQ documents can be used freely as text, it cannot be identified as an NCI PDQ cancer information summary unless it is presented in its entirety and is regularly updated.
